<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41477"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Convergent horizontal gene transfer and cross-talk of mobile nucleic acids in parasitic plants"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Plants 2019 5:9"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2019-07-22"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2019 The Author(s), under exclusive licence to Springer Nature Limited"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Horizontal gene transfer (HGT), the movement and genomic integration of DNA across species boundaries, is commonly associated with bacteria and other microorganisms, but functional HGT (fHGT) is increasingly being recognized in heterotrophic parasitic plants that obtain their nutrients and water from their host plants through direct haustorial feeding. Here, in the holoparasitic stem parasite Cuscuta, we identify 108&#8201;transcribed and probably functional HGT events in Cuscuta campestris and related species, plus 42&#8201;additional regions with host-derived transposon, pseudogene and non-coding sequences. Surprisingly, 18&#8201;Cuscuta fHGTs were acquired from the same gene families by independent HGT events in Orobanchaceae parasites, and the majority are highly expressed in the haustorial feeding structures in both lineages. Convergent retention and expression of HGT sequences suggests an adaptive role for specific additional genes in parasite biology. Between 16 and 20 of the transcribed HGT events are inferred as ancestral in Cuscuta based on transcriptome sequences from species across the phylogenetic range of the genus, implicating fHGT in the successful radiation of Cuscuta parasites. Genome sequencing of C. campestris supports transfer of genomic DNA&#8212;rather than retroprocessed RNA&#8212;as the mechanism of fHGT. Many of the C. campestris genes horizontally acquired are also frequent sources of 24-nucleotide small RNAs that are typically associated with RNA-directed DNA methylation. One HGT encoding a leucine-rich repeat protein kinase overlaps with a microRNA that has been shown to regulate host gene expression, suggesting that HGT-derived parasite small RNAs may function in the parasite&#8211;host interaction. This study enriches our understanding of HGT by describing a parasite&#8211;host system with unprecedented gene exchange that points to convergent evolution of HGT events and the functional importance of horizontally transferred coding and non-coding sequences. A study identified unprecedented horizontal gene transfer (HGT) events in Cuscuta campestris and related species, and provides insights into convergent HGTs between Cuscuta and Orobanchaceae parasites and the functional importance of the HGT sequences."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Horizontal gene transfer (HGT), the movement and genomic integration of DNA across species boundaries, is commonly associated with bacteria and other microorganisms, but functional HGT (fHGT) is increasingly being recognized in heterotrophic parasitic plants that obtain their nutrients and water from their host plants through direct haustorial feeding. Here, in the holoparasitic stem parasite <i>Cuscuta</i>, we identify 108âtranscribed and probably functional HGT events in <i>Cuscuta campestris</i> and related species, plus 42âadditional regions with host-derived transposon, pseudogene and non-coding sequences. Surprisingly, 18â<i>Cuscuta</i> fHGTs were acquired from the same gene families by independent HGT events in Orobanchaceae parasites, and the majority are highly expressed in the haustorial feeding structures in both lineages. Convergent retention and expression of HGT sequences suggests an adaptive role for specific additional genes in parasite biology. Between 16 and 20 of the transcribed HGT events are inferred as ancestral in <i>Cuscuta</i> based on transcriptome sequences from species across the phylogenetic range of the genus, implicating fHGT in the successful radiation of <i>Cuscuta</i> parasites. Genome sequencing of <i>C. campestris</i> supports transfer of genomic DNAârather than retroprocessed RNAâas the mechanism of fHGT. Many of the <i>C. campestris</i> genes horizontally acquired are also frequent sources of 24-nucleotide small RNAs that are typically associated with RNA-directed DNA methylation. One HGT encoding a leucine-rich repeat protein kinase overlaps with a microRNA that has been shown to regulate host gene expression, suggesting that HGT-derived parasite small RNAs may function in the parasiteâhost interaction. This study enriches our understanding of HGT by describing a parasiteâhost system with unprecedented gene exchange that points to convergent evolution of HGT events and the functional importance of horizontally transferred coding and non-coding sequences.</p></div></div></section>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Horizontal gene transfer (HGT), defined as the movement and genomic integration of DNA across species boundaries, is a common mechanism used by bacteria to acquire novel traits such as antibiotic resistance<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Davies, J. &amp; Davies, D. Origins and evolution of antibiotic resistance. Microbiol. Mol. Biol. Rev. 74, 417â433 (2010)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d14571e723">1</a></sup>, but is far less common in plants and other eukaryotes. Although instances of HGT have been documented among autotrophic plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Davis, C. C. &amp; Xi, Z. Horizontal gene transfer in parasitic plants. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 26, 14â19 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d14571e727">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Gao, C. et al. Horizontal gene transfer in plants. Funct. Integr. Genom. 14, 23â29 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d14571e730">3</a></sup>, the process most frequently involves parasitic plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Davis, C. C. &amp; Xi, Z. Horizontal gene transfer in parasitic plants. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 26, 14â19 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d14571e734">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Kado, T. &amp; Innan, H. Horizontal gene transfer in five parasite plant species in Orobanchaceae. Genome Biol. Evol. 10, 3196â3210 (2018)." href="#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d14571e737">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Sun, T. et al. Two hAT transposon genes were transferred from Brassicaceae to broomrapes and are actively expressed in some recipients. Sci. Rep. 6, 30192 (2016)." href="#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d14571e737_1">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Xi, Z. et al. Horizontal transfer of expressed genes in a parasitic flowering plant. BMC Genom. 13, 227 (2012)." href="#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d14571e737_2">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Yang, Z. et al. Horizontal gene transfer is more frequent with increased heterotrophy and contributes to parasite adaptation. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 113, E7010âE7019 (2016)." href="#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d14571e737_3">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Yoshida, S., Maruyama, S., Nozaki, H. &amp; Shirasu, K. Horizontal gene transfer by the parasitic plant Striga hermonthica. Science 328, 1128 (2010)." href="#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d14571e737_4">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Zhang, D. et al. Root parasitic plant Orobanche aegyptiaca and shoot parasitic plant Cuscuta australis obtained Brassicaceae-specific strictosidine synthase-like genes by horizontal gene transfer. BMC Plant Biol. 14, 19 (2014)." href="#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d14571e737_5">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Zhang, Y. et al. Evolution of a horizontally acquired legume gene, albumin 1, in the parasitic plant Phelipanche aegyptiaca and related species. BMC Evol. Biol. 13, 48 (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d14571e740">10</a></sup> probably due to their intimate feeding connections with their host plants. However, the extent of HGT, the mechanisms of transfer and functional implications of the acquired sequences remain unclear. HGT events in parasitic plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Davis, C. C. &amp; Xi, Z. Horizontal gene transfer in parasitic plants. Curr. Opin. Evol. 10, 3196â3210 (2018)." href="#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d14571e747">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Sun, T. et al. Rep. 6, 30192 (2016)." href="#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d14571e747_1">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Xi, Z. et al. Horizontal transfer of expressed genes in a parasitic flowering plant. BMC Genom. 13, 227 (2012)." href="#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d14571e747_2">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Yang, Z. et al. Horizontal gene transfer is more frequent with increased heterotrophy and contributes to parasite adaptation. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 113, E7010âE7019 (2016)." href="#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d14571e747_3">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Yoshida, S., Maruyama, S., Nozaki, H. &amp; Shirasu, K. Horizontal gene transfer by the parasitic plant Striga hermonthica. Science 328, 1128 (2010)." href="#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d14571e747_4">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Zhang, D. et al. BMC Plant Biol. 14, 19 (2014)." href="#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d14571e747_5">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Zhang, Y. et al. Evolution of a horizontally acquired legume gene, albumin 1, in the parasitic plant Phelipanche aegyptiaca and related species. BMC Evol. Biol. 13, 48 (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d14571e750">10</a></sup> have been linked to increasing heterotrophy<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Davis, C. C. &amp; Xi, Z. Horizontal gene transfer in parasitic plants. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 26, 14â19 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d14571e754">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Kado, T. &amp; Innan, H. Horizontal gene transfer in five parasite plant species in Orobanchaceae. Genome Biol. Evol. 10, 3196â3210 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d14571e757">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Yang, Z. et al. Horizontal gene transfer is more frequent with increased heterotrophy and contributes to parasite adaptation. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 113, E7010âE7019 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d14571e760">7</a></sup>, and these horizontally acquired genes may function in parasitic processes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Kado, T. &amp; Innan, H. Horizontal gene transfer in five parasite plant species in Orobanchaceae. Genome Biol. Evol. 10, 3196â3210 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d14571e765">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Yang, Z. et al. Horizontal gene transfer is more frequent with increased heterotrophy and contributes to parasite adaptation. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 113, E7010âE7019 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d14571e768">7</a></sup>. Dodders (genus <i>Cuscuta</i>, Convolvulaceae) are obligate, fully heterotrophic parasites that obtain all carbon and other nutrients from their host plants. In addition, they are twining parasites that attach to host stems and are recognized for having open phloem connections<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Kim, G. &amp; Westwood, J. H. Macromolecule exchange in Cuscuta-host plant interactions. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 26, 20â25 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d14571e775">11</a></sup> with their hosts via the feeding structures known as haustoria, making them ideal systems in which to look for high levels of HGT events<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Vogel, A. et al. Footprints of parasitism in the genome of the parasitic flowering plant Cuscuta campestris. Nat. Commun. 9, 2515 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d14571e779">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Mower, J. P. et al. Horizontal acquisition of multiple mitochondrial genes from a parasitic plant followed by gene conversion with host mitochondrial genes. BMC Biol. 8, 150 (2010)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d14571e782">13</a></sup>. Studies of <i>Cuscutaâ</i>host interactions indicate reciprocal and massive messenger RNA flow (up to 1% of total tissue transcripts) between <i>Cuscuta</i> and its host<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Kim, G., LeBlanc, M. L., Wafula, E., dePamphilis, C. W. &amp; Westwood, J. H. Genomic-scale exchange of mRNA between a parasitic plant and its hosts. Science 345, 808â811 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d14571e793">14</a></sup>. Furthermore, microRNAs are also transferred from <i>Cuscuta campestris</i> to its host and downregulate host mRNAs involved in defence and phloem functions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Shahid, S. et al. MicroRNAs from the parasitic plant Cuscuta campestris target host messenger RNAs. Nature 553, 82â85 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d14571e800">15</a></sup>. We hypothesized that such broad RNA exchange could lead to HGT after reverse transcription of exchanged mRNA, followed by genomic integration of the retroprocessed sequences. One signature of a reverse transcription mechanism of HGT would be the loss of introns in HGT genes compared to donor sequences, and so to infer the presence of introns in HGT sequences we generated a nuclear genome and assembly for <i>C. campestris</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Shahid, S. et al. MicroRNAs from the parasitic plant Cuscuta campestris target host messenger RNAs. Nature 553, 82â85 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d14571e806">15</a></sup>. This approach (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM1">1</a>) has the additional advantage of enabling the identification (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM1">1b</a>) of non-functional pseudogenes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Hepburn, N. J., Schmidt, D. W. &amp; Mower, J. P. Loss of two introns from the Magnolia tripetala mitochondrial cox2 gene implicates horizontal gene transfer and gene conversion as a novel mechanism of intron loss. Mol. Biol. Evol. 29, 3111â3120 (2012)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d14571e817">16</a></sup>, which could result from reverse transcription-mediated HGT and may not be captured by transcriptome data only. A recent initial survey of HGT in <i>C. campestris</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Vogel, A. et al. Footprints of parasitism in the genome of the parasitic flowering plant Cuscuta campestris. Nat. Commun. 9, 2515 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d14571e823">12</a></sup> reported putative HGT events involving 72âgenic regions. We note important methodological differences between the approach used therein and our methods, which we believe yield more true HGT events (for example, caseâ3 in (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM1">2</a>). In this study, we provide systematic approaches for HGT identification and a rigorous pipeline for further validation and analysis of each HGT event. This led to evidence of new phenomena in parasiteâhost interactions, including: convergence of adaptive HGT events in independent parasite lineages, ancestral HGT before species radiation in a large parasitic lineage and evidence that horizontally acquired sequences are sources of mobile small RNAs that may play a role in parasiteâhost interactions.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">A joint BLAST and phylogenomic approach for transcriptome- and genome-based HGT discovery</h3><p>To identify coding HGT events in <i>C. campestris</i>, we used the phylogenomic approach established in ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Yang, Z. et al. Horizontal gene transfer is more frequent with increased heterotrophy and contributes to parasite adaptation. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 113, E7010âE7019 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d14571e845">7</a></sup>, with slightly improved detection strategies (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM1">3</a>), applied to a comprehensive transcriptome from an earlier study of <i>Cuscuta</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Ranjan, A. et al. De novo assembly and characterization of the transcriptome of the parasitic weed dodder identifies genes associated with plant parasitism. Plant Physiol. 166, 1186â1199 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d14571e854">17</a></sup> (now identified as <i>C. campestris</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Shahid, S. et al. MicroRNAs from the parasitic plant Cuscuta campestris target host messenger RNAs. Nature 553, 82â85 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d14571e861">15</a></sup>) (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM1">1a</a>). After rigorous validation, including confirmatory analyses with transcriptomes from eight additional <i>Cuscuta</i> species (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM1">1a</a>), phylogenomic analyses identified 108âhigh-confidence HGT events (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#Fig1">1a</a>), twice the total number of events detected in three parasitic plant genera in Orobanchaceae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Yang, Z. et al. Horizontal gene transfer is more frequent with increased heterotrophy and contributes to parasite adaptation. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 113, E7010âE7019 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d14571e877">7</a></sup> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#Fig1">1a</a>, Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM1">4</a> and Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM3">1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM3">2</a>). A stoichiometry plot where HGT genomic contigs have levels of read coverage and genomic copy content similar to vertically transmitted genomic contigs (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM1">5</a>), plus evidence from quantitative PCR with reverse transcription (RTâPCR) of representative HGT sequences (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM1">6</a>), provide additional support that these HGT sequences are not due to contamination. Unlike <i>Phelipanche</i>, which shows strong bias toward sequences from host lineages in the Rosales (20/40 are from Rosales), the generalist feeder <i>Cuscuta</i> has acquired sequences from a wider range of angiosperm hosts, including Malpighiales, Caryophyllales, Fabales and Malvales (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#Fig1">1b</a> and Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM3">2</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM3">3</a>). Consistent with its current feeding patterns, no HGTs in <i>Cuscuta</i> were derived from grasses (Poales) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#Fig1">1b</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Identification and characterization of HGT genes and donors."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1: Identification and characterization of HGT genes and donors.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-019-0458-0/MediaObjects/41477_2019_458_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-019-0458-0/MediaObjects/41477_2019_458_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="576" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Barplot showing 108âfunctional HGT (fHGT) events of coding genes by phylogenomics (green), and six non-functional HGT-derived pseudogenes and 32ânon-coding elements by genome-based BLAST (light blue) (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM3">4</a>). <b>b</b>, Comparison of HGT results for <i>Cuscuta</i> versus Orobanchaceae. Numbers of HGT events are grouped by donor, to reflect donor preference of each parasitic plant. HGTs from Myrtales (three events), Apiales (three events) and Sapindales (one event) are not shown on this barplot. <b>c</b>, Percentage breakdown of the number of mRNA- and DNA-mediated HGT events (zero RNA-mediated HGTs). Squares with three diagonal marks are exons, horizontal lines between the squares are introns. <b>d</b>, Example of HGT leading to pseudogenes: the presence of introns suggests genomic transfer and accumulation of premature stop codons, indicating that some HGT sequences are subject to pseudogenization.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To complement phylogenomically based HGT identification of protein-coding sequences but, in particular, to explore the possibility of reverse transcription-mediated HGTs, we applied a BLAST-based approach (HGTpropor) to the sequenced nuclear genome to identify pseudogenized or non-coding HGTs (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM1">1b</a>). This method identifies HGTs based on the proportion of strong BLAST alignments (hits) from distantly related organisms compared to closely related taxonomic lineages. This method identified a minimum of 41âhost-derived non-coding HGTs (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM3">2</a>), including (1) HGT-derived pseudogenes from six gene families (OrthoFinder groups; five of six OrthoFinder groups encode retrotransposon-related proteins in the donor genome) and (2) 32âplant-derived non-coding HGT scaffolds, the majority of which (24 out of 32) are Fabales/Brassicales-derived long terminal repeat (LTR) elements including 24âTy1/Copia and one Gypsy/DIRS1 (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#Fig1">1a</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM3">2</a>). While the method has been tuned to be conservative, it also identifies, when applied to coding regions, a large subset of the HGTs (59âOrthoFinder groups, 74âevents) predicted by phylogenomics (Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM1">1</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM1">4</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM1">7a</a> and Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM3">4</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM3">5</a>). Moreover, HGTpropor identified two published HGTs (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM3">6</a>)âalbuminâ1 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Zhang, Y. et al. Evolution of a horizontally acquired legume gene, albumin 1, in the parasitic plant Phelipanche aegyptiaca and related species. BMC Evol. Biol. 13, 48 (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d14571e994">10</a></sup> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM1">8</a>) and SSL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Zhang, D. et al. Root parasitic plant Orobanche aegyptiaca and shoot parasitic plant Cuscuta australis obtained Brassicaceae-specific strictosidine synthase-like genes by horizontal gene transfer. BMC Plant Biol. 14, 19 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d14571e1002">9</a></sup> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM1">9</a>).</p><p>Because we have tried to establish a high bar for HGT acceptance through stringent criteria and multiple cross-checks, it is likely that we have rejected some true HGTs that lacked sufficient evidence. Moreover, additional HGTs may still be discovered with additional transcriptome and/or genome sequencing of the different <i>Cuscuta</i> species, and with continued expansion of the sequenced plant database.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Protein-coding HGTs are under evolutionary constraint</h3><p>To test whether HGT sequences are evolving under constraint and likely to encode functional proteins, we used the branch test in PAML<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Yang, Z. PAML 4: phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 1586â1591 (2007)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d14571e1023">18</a></sup> for evolutionary analyses of the 108âHGT events, in which HGT sequences from <i>C. campestris</i> and related <i>Cuscuta</i> species on each phylogenetic tree were used as the foreground. The overwhelming majority of protein-coding HGT sequences in <i>Cuscuta</i> are evolving either under selective constraint similar to the background levels (69âgenes) or have experienced even stronger levels of purifying selection (34âgenes) (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM3">7</a>). Three <i>Cuscuta</i> HGT lineages display relaxed purifying selection compared to the background (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM3">7</a>), and two suggest positive selection (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM3">7</a>). This analysis provides another line of evidence supporting functionality of the transcribed HGT sequences in <i>Cuscuta</i>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Defence response and mRNA processing are enriched among the HGT genes in <i>Cuscuta</i>
                        </h3><p>To infer the potential roles of the horizontally acquired genes in <i>Cuscuta</i>, we performed Gene Ontology<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Gene Ontology Consortium. Gene Ontology Consortium: going forward. Nucleic Acids Res. 43, D1049âD1056 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d14571e1067">19</a></sup> enrichment analyses of the 108âtranscribed and functional HGTs. We found that defence response genes (Gene Ontology biological process (BP)) are significantly enriched (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM3">8</a>) (Fisherâs exact test, <i>P</i>â=â1.69âÃâ10<sup>â4</sup>). Other enriched terms included protein phosphorylation (<i>P</i>â=â6.27âÃâ10<sup>â8</sup>) and leucine-rich repeat domains (<i>P</i>â=â3.36âÃâ10<sup>â8</sup>), which are also commonly associated with defence responses<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Park, C. J., Caddell, D. F. &amp; Ronald, P. C. Protein phosphorylation in plant immunity: insights into the regulation of pattern recognition receptor-mediated signaling. Front. Plant Sci. 3, 177 (2012)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d14571e1090">20</a></sup> (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM3">8</a>). Another enriched GOSlim BP category is amino acid metabolic processes (<i>P</i>â=â0.047), which may be linked to enriched Interproscan terms such as aminoacyl transfer RNA synthetase (<i>P</i>â=â0.02) (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM3">8</a>). Among the non-coding HGT set, retrotransposase and LTR retrotransposon sequences are mostly represented among the HGT pseudogenes and non-coding repetitive elements, respectively (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM3">2</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Functional and non-functional HGTâDNA not mRNA</h3><p>Abundant mRNA transfer between <i>Cuscuta</i> and its host<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Kim, G., LeBlanc, M. L., Wafula, E., dePamphilis, C. W. &amp; Westwood, J. H. Genomic-scale exchange of mRNA between a parasitic plant and its hosts. Science 345, 808â811 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d14571e1121">14</a></sup> led us to hypothesize that mobile RNA could be an important source of HGT in this parasite. However, introns were detected in all of the transcribed and evolutionarily constrained HGT genes where the donor sequences contained introns (90âfHGT events) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#Fig1">1c</a>). The absence of any detected intron losses argues against a mRNA-mediated mechanism for fHGTs in <i>Cuscuta</i>. To investigate this further, we randomly selected ten gene families to compare the intron positions and sequence from the HGT gene, the inferred donor and the vertical relative. Due to their usually short length, and difficulty of homology assessment in gappy alignments, intron phylogenies are typically poorly resolved. Similar to HGT in Orobanchaceae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Yang, Z. et al. Horizontal gene transfer is more frequent with increased heterotrophy and contributes to parasite adaptation. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 113, E7010âE7019 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d14571e1131">7</a></sup>, the intron positions are largely highly conserved across the sampled angiosperms, including donor, recipient and related lineages. In one case where the donor and acceptor sites GT and AG could be identified (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM1">10</a>), comparison of the sequences confirmed a match between the donor and parasite, and not the vertical relative, providing structural confirmation of a genomic transfer (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM1">10</a>). We then considered whether site-specific homing introns may have invaded intron-less HGT sequences after insertion. An example of such an invasive sequence that is known in parasitic plants is the TypeâI homing intron of probable fungal origin<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Cho, Y., Qiu, Y. L., Kuhlman, P. &amp; Palmer, J. D. Explosive invasion of plant mitochondria by a group I intron. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 95, 14244â14249 (1998)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d14571e1142">21</a></sup> that has repeatedly invaded the mitochondrial <i>coxI</i> gene of parasitic plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Barkman, T. J. et al. Mitochondrial DNA suggests at least 11 origins of parasitism in angiosperms and reveals genomic chimerism in parasitic plants. BMC Evol. Biol. 7, 248 (2007)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d14571e1149">22</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Cho, Y., Mower, J. P., Qiu, Y. L. &amp; Palmer, J. D. Mitochondrial substitution rates are extraordinarily elevated and variable in a genus of flowering plants. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 101, 17741â17746 (2004)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d14571e1152">23</a></sup>. However, a search for functional annotations associated with each of the 729âintrons contained in the HGT genes in this study found only a single intron sequence with the annotation term Endonuclease-reverse transcriptase (intron from Seq716_Cp_v0.1_Contig94547_16048). There were no occurrences of other annotation terms that would also have suggested a potential homing intron (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#Sec11">Methods</a>). These lines of evidence all point to <i>Cuscuta</i> fHGTs resulting overwhelmingly from transfer of intact genomic DNA, including introns. Such sequences are much more likely than randomly inserted retroprocessed sequences to have recognizable promoter elements in the new genomic environment, greatly increasing the likelihood that a foreign sequence would be transcribed and find function in the recipient parasite<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Yang, Z. et al. Horizontal gene transfer is more frequent with increased heterotrophy and contributes to parasite adaptation. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 113, E7010âE7019 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d14571e1163">7</a></sup>.</p><p>Although genomic integration is the apparent mechanism for fHGT events in <i>Cuscuta</i>, we hypothesized that reverse transcription of abundantly transferred mRNA in this species might lead to an accumulation of unexpressed pseudogenes, because reverse-transcribed mRNAs would usually be incorporated into genomes without functional regulatory modules. However, in the majority of HGT-derived pseudogenes we examined (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM3">2</a>) that mostly encode retrotransposons or retrotransposon-domains, an absence of introns in the donor sequence made it impossible either to support or refute a reverse transcription-mediated hypothesis. In just one instance, we observed conserved introns in an HGT sequence that were similar to those in the donor gene, indicating a genomic transfer (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#Fig1">1d</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM1">11</a>). The presence of multiple premature stop codons suggests that this sequence is a pseudogene (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#Fig1">1d</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM1">11</a>). In another case of retrotransposon HGT, we observed both non-functional and potentially fHGT copies coexisting in the <i>Cuscuta</i> genome (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM1">12</a>).</p><p>Further refuting the possibility of reverse transcription-mediated transfer, we compared our list of HGT genes to a dataset of host-to-<i>Cuscuta</i> mobile mRNA and found no enrichment in HGT genes among these mRNAs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Kim, G., LeBlanc, M. L., Wafula, E., dePamphilis, C. W. &amp; Westwood, J. H. Genomic-scale exchange of mRNA between a parasitic plant and its hosts. Science 345, 808â811 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d14571e1201">14</a></sup> (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM3">9</a>). In contrast, we found that HGT genes from 23âorthogroups generated <i>Cuscuta</i>-to-host mobile mRNAs (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM3">10</a>). These include mobile HGT genes that encode disease resistance proteins such as NB-ARC domain-containing disease resistance protein, cell wall-modifying enzymes including beta-glucosidaseâ24, and leucine-transfer RNA ligase. Although we find no evidence for a role of mobile mRNA as a source of HGT, transferred genes in the parasite express mRNAs that move back into the host and could affect parasiteâhost interactions (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM3">8</a>). We conclude that mobile DNA, rather than mobile mRNA, has been the primary contributor to HGT in <i>Cuscuta</i>. Questions regarding the fate and function of trans-species mobile mRNAs will need to be addressed in future research.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">HGT displays functional and transcriptional convergence in two parasitic lineages</h3><p>To examine whether certain types of foreign genes may be favoured for retention in parasitic species, we compared the list of <i>Cuscuta</i>-coding HGTs to those from Orobanchaceae parasites<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Yang, Z. et al. Horizontal gene transfer is more frequent with increased heterotrophy and contributes to parasite adaptation. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 113, E7010âE7019 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d14571e1233">7</a></sup>. Among 96âHGT-derived OrthoFinder groups in <i>Cuscuta</i>, phylogenetic trees of 18âOrthoFinder groups support independent HGT events in Orobanchaceae parasites (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#Fig2">2a</a>). For instance, independent horizontal acquisitions (Shimodaira and Hasegawa (SH) test, <i>P</i>â&lt;â0.01) of a leucine-tRNA ligase occurred in <i>Cuscuta</i> and <i>Phelipanche</i> from Malvales and Malpighiales, respectively (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#Fig2">2b</a>). The overall probability of an HGT event in any given gene family in <i>Cuscuta</i> is approximately 0.49% (96/19,470 orthogroups), and in Orobanchaceae 0.25% (49/19,470). The probability of independent HGT events occurring in both of these lineages is very low (0.0012%â=â0.49âÃâ0.25%). Thus, the retention of 18âshared HGT OrthoFinder groups is extremely unlikely (Fisherâs exact test, <i>P</i>âââ2.2âÃâ10<sup>â16</sup>) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#Fig2">2a</a>). Moreover, at least half of the HGT genes in <i>Cuscuta</i> were highly expressed in haustorial tissues (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#Fig2">2c</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM3">11</a>). Of the expressed HGT genes shared by both <i>Cuscuta</i> and Orobanchaceae, 14 were expressed in haustoria and two were not (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#Fig2">2d</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM3">11</a>) (one-sided Fisherâs test, <i>P</i>â=â0.046). A previous study of Orobanchaceae parasites revealed HGT of four haustorial-upregulated tRNA synthetases in <i>Phelipanche</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Yang, Z. et al. Horizontal gene transfer is more frequent with increased heterotrophy and contributes to parasite adaptation. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 113, E7010âE7019 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d14571e1292">7</a></sup>. We found an enrichment of aminoacyl tRNA synthetases in <i>Cuscuta</i> HGTs (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM3">8</a>). We examined the HGT gene topology and transcriptional profile in <i>Cuscuta</i> and Orobanchaceae parasites for the 18âshared HGT trees (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#Fig2">2a</a>). Six OrthoFinder trees support strong convergent evolution where the HGT genes in both lineages show similar expression and independent transfer events (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM3">12</a>). Together with their upregulation in haustorial tissues<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Yang, Z. et al. Horizontal gene transfer is more frequent with increased heterotrophy and contributes to parasite adaptation. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 113, E7010âE7019 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d14571e1312">7</a></sup> (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM3">11</a>), this suggests that these HGT gene products could contribute to metabolic capacity and/or parasitic ability of the haustoria in the two parasitic lineages. Thus, we see evidence for widespread convergent acquisition of HGT genes, linking HGT gene function and expression through differential HGT survival and acquisition of haustorial expression.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Convergent evolution of expressed HGTs in two independent parasitic lineages."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: Convergent evolution of expressed HGTs in two independent parasitic lineages.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-019-0458-0/MediaObjects/41477_2019_458_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-019-0458-0/MediaObjects/41477_2019_458_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="968" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Fisherâs exact test (total number of genes, <i>n</i>â=â19,564) to assess the significance of co-occurrence of orthogroups containing HGT events in both <i>Cuscuta</i> and Orobanchaceae lineages. The expected value for one HGT occurring in both lineages is 0.2. <b>b</b>, HGT tree (60âsequences) showing two separate events in <i>Cuscuta</i> and <i>Phelipanche</i> (SH test, <i>P</i>â&lt;â0.01). HGT sequences in <i>Cuscuta</i> and <i>Phelipanche</i> are labelled with yellow highlighting and as H1 and H2, respectively. Gene sequences annotated in the genome are highlighted in orange. Vertically transmitted sequences are labelled with green highlighting and V. Clades H and V contain parasitic species in the genus <i>Cuscuta</i> and the Orobanchaceae parasites <i>P. aegyptiaca</i>, <i>S. hermonthica</i> and <i>T. versicolor</i>. <b>c</b>, Heatmap showing the transcriptional profile (the expression of each gene from each tissue involves <i>n</i>â=â2 replicates) of all <i>Cuscuta</i> HGTs (178âsequences). Genes with high expression in haustorial tissues are denoted as haustorial expression, and the remainder as non-haustorial. <b>d</b>, One-sided Fisherâs exact test (total number of genes, <i>n</i>â=â86) to test for over-representation of HGT genes with haustorial expression occurring in two lineages. <b>e</b>, Hypothetical tree illustrating the colour-coding system of the major angiosperm lineages in Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#Fig2">2b</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#Fig3">3g</a>.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Despite the striking similarity in events between <i>Cuscuta</i> and Orobanchaceae, we see evidence of HGT genes encoding cell wall-modifying enzymes that were captured by an ancestor of all sampled <i>Cuscuta</i> species (Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM1">13</a>â<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM1">15</a>), but not by Orobanchaceae parasites<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Kado, T. &amp; Innan, H. Horizontal gene transfer in five parasite plant species in Orobanchaceae. Genome Biol. Evol. 10, 3196â3210 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d14571e1419">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Yang, Z. et al. Horizontal gene transfer is more frequent with increased heterotrophy and contributes to parasite adaptation. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 113, E7010âE7019 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d14571e1422">7</a></sup>. Two of these HGT-derived cell wall-modifying enzymes (glycosyl hydrolase and pectin acetylesterase) are expressed primarily in prehaustorial structures (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM1">13</a>), implicating a role in host invasion.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">HGTs as sources of mobile small RNAs</h3><p>Given recent evidence that <i>Cuscuta</i>-derived miRNAs target host gene expression<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Shahid, S. et al. MicroRNAs from the parasitic plant Cuscuta campestris target host messenger RNAs. Nature 553, 82â85 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d14571e1441">15</a></sup>, we hypothesized that HGT could be a source of host-specific small RNAs. This was supported by the discovery of many HGT genes in <i>Cuscuta</i> encoding leucine-rich repeat (LRR) domain-containing proteins (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM3">2</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM3">8</a>), which are known to generate miRNA-dependent short interfering RNAs that may act in silencing cascades<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Li, F. et al. MicroRNA regulation of plant innate immune receptors. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 109, 1790â1795 (2012)." href="#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d14571e1455">24</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Fei, Q., Xia, R. &amp; Meyers, B. C. Phased, secondary, small interfering RNAs in posttranscriptional regulatory networks. Plant Cell 25, 2400â2415 (2013)." href="#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d14571e1455_1">25</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Arikit, S. et al. An atlas of soybean small RNAs identifies phased siRNAs from hundreds of coding genes. Plant Cell 26, 4584â4601 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d14571e1458">26</a></sup>. We also found several transposable element-related HGTs and repeat-derived HGT fragments (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM3">2</a>). Repetitive DNA and transposable elements are known to be rich sources of 24-nucleotide (nt) siRNAs that target their silencing via the RNA-dependent DNA methylation pathway<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Law, J. A. &amp; Jacobsen, S. E. Establishing, maintaining and modifying DNA methylation patterns in plants and animals. Nat. Rev. Genet. 11, 204â220 (2010)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d14571e1465">27</a></sup>. To evaluate this hypothesis, we compared the HGT set to previously annotated small RNA loci in <i>C. campestris</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Shahid, S. et al. MicroRNAs from the parasitic plant Cuscuta campestris target host messenger RNAs. Nature 553, 82â85 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d14571e1471">15</a></sup>. We found that 75 out of 200â<i>C. campestris</i> protein-coding HGT genes (the combined set of HGT sequences identified from phylogenomic and BLAST approaches, shown in Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM3">2</a>) overlap with 147âsmall RNA loci (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#Fig3">3a</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM3">13</a>), significantly higher than the non-HGT background (right-tailed Chi-square test, <i>P</i>â=â1.01âÃâ10<sup>â5</sup>) (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM3">13</a>). This suggests that HGT sequences are more likely to be small RNA sources compared to canonical genes. The majority of the overlapping small RNA loci (119 out of 147) produce small RNAs from both strands (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#Fig3">3a</a>), indicating these are siRNA loci. In the case of non-coding HGT fragments and pseudogenes, only five out of 43 overlap with small RNA-producing loci (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM1">16b</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM3">14</a>). Most of the protein-coding, HGT-overlapping small RNA loci (138/147) predominantly generate 24-nt siRNAs (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#Fig3">3b</a>, Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM1">16a</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM3">13</a>). Besides LRR genes and transposable elements, many other protein-coding HGTs are also associated with 24-nt siRNA-producing regions (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM1">17</a>). Most intriguingly, one of the HGT genes in <i>Cuscuta</i>, which encodes a LRR protein kinase (<i>Cc_v0.1_Contig437972_11875</i>) highly expressed in haustoria (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#Fig3">3g</a>), overlaps with a known <i>MIRNA</i>, <i>ccm-MIR12494a</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Shahid, S. et al. MicroRNAs from the parasitic plant Cuscuta campestris target host messenger RNAs. Nature 553, 82â85 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d14571e1534">15</a></sup> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#Fig3">3c</a>). Specifically, the 3ââUTR of the HGT gene overlaps with the 3ââend of this <i>MIRNA</i> locus by 24âbase pairs (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#Fig3">3c,d</a>). <i>ccm-MIR12494a</i> has previously been shown to be induced in the haustoria (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#Fig3">3e</a>), where it triggers cleavage of a host mRNAâ<i>Arabidopsis Heat Shock Factor Binding 4/Schizoriza</i> (<i>HSFB4/SCZ</i>) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#Fig3">3f</a>)âfollowed by secondary siRNA production<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Shahid, S. et al. MicroRNAs from the parasitic plant Cuscuta campestris target host messenger RNAs. Nature 553, 82â85 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d14571e1563">15</a></sup>. In <i>Arabidopsis</i>, <i>SCZ</i> regulates the determination of stem cell fate in ground tissues<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="ten Hove, C. A. et al. SCHIZORIZA encodes a nuclear factor regulating asymmetry of stem cell divisions in the Arabidopsis root. Curr. Biol. 20, 452â457 (2010)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d14571e1574">28</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Mylona, P., Linstead, P., Martienssen, R. &amp; Dolan, L. SCHIZORIZA controls an asymmetric cell division and restricts epidermal identity in the Arabidopsis root. Development 129, 4327â4334 (2002)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d14571e1577">29</a></sup> and de novo formation of roots<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Bustillo-Avendano, E. et al. Regulation of hormonal control, cell reprogramming, and patterning during de novo root organogenesis. Plant Physiol. 176, 1709â1727 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d14571e1581">30</a></sup>. The phylogenetic tree supports an HGT in the common ancestor of all <i>Cuscuta</i> spp. from a Rosales lineage closely related to <i>Ziziphus jujuba</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Liu, M. J. et al. The complex jujube genome provides insights into fruit tree biology. Nat. Commun. 5, 5315 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d14571e1590">31</a></sup> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#Fig3">3g</a>). Although we cannot be certain of the exact boundaries of this HGT sequence given the fact that this miRNA family is not known outside of <i>Cuscuta</i>, we favour the hypothesis that it arose after the HGT event, perhaps evolving from the HGT-inserted sequence itself, given their close proximity. This suggests that a trans-species active miRNA is very closely linked to, if not part of, an ancient HGT event.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="C. campestris HGT events are a frequent source of small RNAs."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: <i>C. campestris</i> HGT events are a frequent source of small RNAs.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-019-0458-0/MediaObjects/41477_2019_458_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-019-0458-0/MediaObjects/41477_2019_458_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="1284" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Scatter plot showing percentage of <i>C. campestris</i> HGT genes or random gene sets (tenârandom samplings of 200âgenes per sampling) that enriched for overlap with small RNA-producing loci. <b>b</b>, Predominant RNA size and strandedness of HGT-overlapping small RNA loci. Here, the symbols +, â and Â± indicate plus, minus and both strands of the genome, respectively. <b>c</b>, Structure of a HGT gene which overlaps with a known <i>MIRNA</i> locus (<i>ccm-MIR12494a</i>). <b>d</b>, Small RNA coverage along the hairpin structure of <i>ccm-MIR12494a</i>. <b>e</b>, Heatmap showing the expression of <i>ccm-MIR12494a</i> induced in haustoria. RPM, reads per million. <b>f</b>, Diagram illustrating how HGT-overlapping <i>ccm-MIR12494a</i> functions in parasiteâhost interactions. <b>g</b>, Phylogenetic tree (55âsequences) supporting the occurrence of HGT in a common ancestor of eight <i>Cuscuta</i> species from a Rosales ancestor. HGT sequences are highlighted in yellow and by H; vertically transmitted sequences are highlighted in light blue and by V. Heatmap (<i>n</i>â=â2 replicates from each tissue) showing the primary haustorial expression of two HGT sequences.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Given that HGT genes were foreign when they first entered the <i>Cuscuta</i> genome, we speculate that some HGT-associated siRNAs may have been generated as part of a genomic silencing response against the incoming DNA segments, which can often cause local repeat structures that trigger siRNA production. For instance, repetitive pararetroviral sequences integrated into the genome of the flowering plant <i>Fritillaria imperialis</i> have been shown to generate 24-nt siRNAs that target these for silencing<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Becher, H. et al. Endogenous pararetrovirus sequences associated with 24 nt small RNAs at the centromeres of Fritillaria imperialis L. (Liliaceae), a species with a giant genome. Plant J. 80, 823â833 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d14571e1679">32</a></sup>. It is also possible that some HGT-associated <i>Cuscuta</i> small RNAs, such as <i>ccm-MIR12494a</i>, may have been acquired as part of adaptation to parasitism.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">HGT in <i>Cuscuta</i> is ancestral and an ongoing process</h3><p>Nine horizontally acquired genes were identified in the transcriptomes of a genus-wide set of eight <i>Cuscuta</i> taxa sampled in this study, but absent from non-parasitic Convolvulaceae and related families, and are thus inferred to have been acquired in an ancestral <i>Cuscuta</i> parasite (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#Fig4">4</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM3">15</a>). By mapping the presence of the detected HGT sequences onto the reconstructed species tree, the number and timing of HGT events occurring at each node of the tree were reconstructed with two methods that accommodate intron loss or missing data<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Costea, M., GarcÃ­a, M. A., Baute, K. &amp; StefanoviÄ, S. Entangled evolutionary history of Cuscuta pentagona clade: a story involving hybridization and Darwin in the Galapagos. Taxon 64, 1225â1242 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d14571e1713">33</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Costea, M., GarcÃ­a, M. A. &amp; StefanoviÄ, S. A phylogenetically based infrageneric classification of the parasitic plant genus Cuscuta (Dodders, Convolvulaceae). Syst. Bot. 40, 269â285 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d14571e1716">34</a></sup>. In this way, between seven and 11 additional HGT events were inferred as ancestral in our genus-wide sampling (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#Fig4">4</a>) (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM3">15</a>). This is very different from previous observations of HGT in Orobanchaceae, where no HGTs were found to be ancestral<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Yang, Z. et al. Horizontal gene transfer is more frequent with increased heterotrophy and contributes to parasite adaptation. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 113, E7010âE7019 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d14571e1727">7</a></sup>. The 16â20 ancestral <i>Cuscuta</i> HGT genes include the previously discovered albumin1 (refs. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Zhang, Y. et al. Evolution of a horizontally acquired legume gene, albumin 1, in the parasitic plant Phelipanche aegyptiaca and related species. BMC Evol. Biol. 13, 48 (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d14571e1734">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Vogel, A. et al. Footprints of parasitism in the genome of the parasitic flowering plant Cuscuta campestris. Nat. Commun. 9, 2515 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d14571e1737">12</a></sup>), LRR protein kinase, cell wall-modifying enzymes such as the glycoside hydrolase family, and transposons (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM3">16</a>). The largest number of surviving HGT events occurred in an ancestor of <i>Cuscuta chilensis</i> and the five other members of subgenus <i>Grammica</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#Fig4">4</a>). Three of the ancestral HGTs encode transposable element gene families (hAT transposons), with the level of sequence identity among <i>Cuscuta</i> spp. falling within the range of identities seen in the ancestral protein-coding genes (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM3">16</a>). In addition, the ratios of synonymous and non-synonymous substitutions per site for the two transposable element-related orthogroups show the proteins evolving under strong and weak purifying selection (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM3">7</a>). Furthermore, phylogenies of the two transposable element genes (OrthoFinder groupâ29 in Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM1">4.11</a> and OrthoFinder groupâ31 in Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM1">4.13</a>) are very close to the <i>Cuscuta</i> species phylogeny. These lines of evidence together support the inference that these were indeed ancestral insertions as implied by the formal character reconstructions. The distribution of HGT events in various ancestral and terminal lineages indicates that HGT has been an ongoing process in <i>Cuscuta</i>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="HGT is both ancestral and an ongoing process in Cuscuta."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4: HGT is both ancestral and an ongoing process in <i>Cuscuta</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-019-0458-0/MediaObjects/41477_2019_458_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-019-0458-0/MediaObjects/41477_2019_458_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="337" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>The HGT states of each ancestral node of the <i>Cuscuta</i> species tree<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Costea, M., GarcÃ­a, M. A., Baute, K. &amp; StefanoviÄ, S. Entangled evolutionary history of Cuscuta pentagona clade: a story involving hybridization and Darwin in the Galapagos. Taxon 64, 1225â1242 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d14571e1795">33</a></sup> were inferred with Dollo and Wagner parsimony (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM3">15</a>).
