The inferred numbers of HGT events for each ancestral and terminal node are labelled with two sets of bars, representing each reconstruction method. The inferred numbers of HGT gain-and-loss events (which could represent unexpressed genes in the tissues sampled) are labelled within each bar.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><p>Our analyses of HGT in <i>Cuscuta</i> led to the following conclusions.</p><ol class="u-list-style-none">
                  <li>
                    <span class="u-custom-list-number">(1)</span>
                    
                      <p>As predicted by its heterotrophic habit and open vascular connections, <i>Cuscuta</i> has experienced high levels of host-to-parasite horizontal gene transfer. The markedly higher incidence of HGT in <i>Cuscuta</i> compared to holoparasitic Orobanchaceae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Kado, T. &amp; Innan, H. Horizontal gene transfer in five parasite plant species in Orobanchaceae. Genome Biol. Evol. 10, 3196â3210 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d14571e1838">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Yang, Z. et al. Horizontal gene transfer is more frequent with increased heterotrophy and contributes to parasite adaptation. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 113, E7010âE7019 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d14571e1841">7</a></sup> could have occurred because <i>Cuscutaâs</i> numerous stem haustorial connections, distributed throughout the vine, are physically much closer to the floral meristematic tissues than in the root-parasitic Orobanchaceae, making the path shorter for integration of host DNA transported through the haustorium into cells that ultimately enter the parasite germline.</p>
                    
                  </li>
                  <li>
                    <span class="u-custom-list-number">(2)</span>
                    
                      <p>Although large numbers of fHGTs have been discovered through analyses of transcribed gene sets in <i>Cuscuta</i>, we also identified numerous HGT-derived pseudogenes, transposon-derived sequences and non-coding elements in the <i>Cuscuta</i> genome. Despite the large number of documented HGT events, the genome of <i>C. campestris</i> is a fairly modest 581âmega base pairs (bp) per amount of DNA contained within an unreplicated haploid chromosome set (1C)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Vogel, A. et al. Footprints of parasitism in the genome of the parasitic flowering plant Cuscuta campestris. Nat. Commun. 9, 2515 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d14571e1868">12</a></sup>, and the amount of xenologous sequence detected to date in this species is small relative to the total genome size. However, other parasitic plants have greatly expanded genomes that are far larger than those of closely related parasitic or non-parasitic relatives<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="McNeal, J. R., Kuehl, J. V., Boore, J. L. &amp; dePamphilis, C. W. Complete plastid genome sequences suggest strong selection for retention of photosynthetic genes in the parasitic plant genus Cuscuta. BMC Plant Biol. 7, 57 (2007)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d14571e1872">35</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Westwood, J. H., Yoder, J. I., Timko, M. P. &amp; dePamphilis, C. W. The evolution of parasitism in plants. Trends Plant Sci. 15, 227â235 (2010)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d14571e1875">36</a></sup>. Parasites with giant genomes include certain Orobanchaceae (up to 19âGbp per 1C)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Weiss-Schneeweiss, H., Greilhuber, J. &amp; Schneeweiss, G. M. Genome size evolution in holoparasitic Orobanche (Orobanchaceae) and related genera. Am. J. Bot. 93, 148â156 (2006)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d14571e1880">37</a></sup>, <i>Cuscuta indecora</i> (32âGbpâper 1C)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="McNeal, J. R., Kuehl, J. V., Boore, J. L. &amp; dePamphilis, C. W. Complete plastid genome sequences suggest strong selection for retention of photosynthetic genes in the parasitic plant genus Cuscuta. BMC Plant Biol. 7, 57 (2007)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d14571e1887">35</a></sup> and certain mistletoes (up to 80âGbpâper 1C, or 27 times larger than a human genome!)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Zonneveld, B. J. M. New record holders for maximum genome size in eudicots and monocots. J. Bot. 2010, 527357 (2010)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d14571e1891">38</a></sup> (<a href="http://data.kew.org/cvalues/">http://data.kew.org/cvalues/</a>). We hypothesize that horizontal transfers of foreign sequences into parasitic plants expose them to host-derived transposable elements that can sometimes expand wildly in their new genomic environment. Most transposable elements, of course, are quiescent due to epigenetic silencing<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Fultz, D., Choudury, S. G. &amp; Slotkin, R. K. Silencing of active transposable elements in plants. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 27, 67â76 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d14571e1902">39</a></sup> or mutation. If either active or silenced transposable elements in the host genome are acquired via horizontal transfer into the parasite<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Sun, T. et al. Two hAT transposon genes were transferred from Brassicaceae to broomrapes and are actively expressed in some recipients. Sci. Rep. 6, 30192 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d14571e1907">40</a></sup>, they may in some cases be able to replicate rapidly outside of epigenetic control in the ânaiveâ parasitic plant genome, allowing them to expand greatly before silencing is achieved. Such an event of HGT-mediated âescapeâ into the parasitic plant genome would be highly adaptive for transposons that are silenced but potentially functional. If true, fHGT could be just the âtip of the icebergâ of the total amount of foreign DNA in the vastly larger genomes of some parasitic plants. Future sequencing of these giant genomes will be needed to test this prediction.</p>
                    
                  </li>
                  <li>
                    <span class="u-custom-list-number">(3)</span>
                    
                      <p>Genes that have been acquired by <i>Cuscuta</i> through HGT are enriched for haustorial expression, defence response and amino acid metabolism, suggesting that fHGT contributes to the mechanism of parasiteâhost interactions. This process of acquiring functional genes that could contribute to parasitic ability began early in the history of <i>Cuscuta</i>, with 16â20âhorizontally acquired genes having been retained from a common ancestor of extant <i>Cuscuta</i> species. Two independent parasitic lineages underwent convergent evolution after acquiring genes of similar functional categories through independent HGT events, with many genes evolving predominantly haustorial expression, supporting the notion that HGT has indeed been used as a mechanism for enhancing parasiteâhost interactions.</p>
                    
                  </li>
                  <li>
                    <span class="u-custom-list-number">(4)</span>
                    
                      <p><i>Cuscuta</i> HGTs are enriched as sources for endogenous siRNAs associated with silencing. This suggests that incoming HGT sequences might be recognized as foreign, and targeted for silencing. However, despite this targeting, the HGT genes we discovered are clearly expressed and are generally evolving under constraint as functional proteins. The overlap of an HGT event with a miRNA that targets host mRNAs suggests that HGT sequences could also spawn novel regulatory functions to promote parasite success. Collectively, these lines of evidence suggest that, in <i>Cuscuta</i>, HGT not only contributes to a parasitic lifestyle but modulates parasiteâhost interaction by interacting with mobile mRNA/sRNAs.</p>
                    
                  </li>
                  <li>
                    <span class="u-custom-list-number">(5)</span>
                    
                      <p>Our study identifies evidence of cross-talk between mobile DNA (HGT), mobile mRNAs and mobile small RNAs (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#Fig5">5</a>). We demonstrated that genomic DNA from the host, rather than mRNA, acts as the source of parasite HGTs. In addition, nuclear HGTs in parasitic <i>Cuscuta</i> have previously been detected only in the direction from host to parasite, and not from parasite to host as in our analyses (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#Fig5">5</a>). DNA fragments moving from host to parasite can exist in the form of genes or non-coding elements such as transposable elements and repeats. Following horizontal transfer, these HGTs are subject to both selective retention and evolution by mutation, which can give rise to pseudogenes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#Fig5">5</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="HGT pathways in light of interaction with mobile mRNAs and mobile small RNAs."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5: HGT pathways in light of interaction with mobile mRNAs and mobile small RNAs.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-019-0458-0/MediaObjects/41477_2019_458_Fig5_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-019-0458-0/MediaObjects/41477_2019_458_Fig5_HTML.png" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="288" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>Blue represents parasite, purple represents host. Solid lines represent direct evidence, dashed lines represent potential pathways that currently lack evidence in this system (but see ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Mower, J. P. et al. Horizontal acquisition of multiple mitochondrial genes from a parasitic plant followed by gene conversion with host mitochondrial genes. BMC Biol. 8, 150 (2010)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d14571e1982">13</a></sup> for a probable case of parasite-to-host HGT in plant mitochondrial DNA). Numbers on branches represent supporting reference numbers, and the blue asterisk represents a group of five references (refs. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Davis, C. C. &amp; Xi, Z. Horizontal gene transfer in parasitic plants. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 26, 14â19 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d14571e1986">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Yang, Z. et al. Horizontal gene transfer is more frequent with increased heterotrophy and contributes to parasite adaptation. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 113, E7010âE7019 (2016)." href="#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d14571e1989">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Yoshida, S., Maruyama, S., Nozaki, H. &amp; Shirasu, K. Horizontal gene transfer by the parasitic plant Striga hermonthica. Science 328, 1128 (2010)." href="#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d14571e1989_1">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Zhang, D. et al. Root parasitic plant Orobanche aegyptiaca and shoot parasitic plant Cuscuta australis obtained Brassicaceae-specific strictosidine synthase-like genes by horizontal gene transfer. BMC Plant Biol. 14, 19 (2014)." href="#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d14571e1989_2">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Zhang, Y. et al. Evolution of a horizontally acquired legume gene, albumin 1, in the parasitic plant Phelipanche aegyptiaca and related species. BMC Evol. Biol. 13, 48 (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d14571e1992">10</a></sup>). DNA-mediated HGT from host to parasite is the predominant form; HGT from parasite to host is rare and seldom results in fHGT. mRNA-mediated HGT has not been observed in <i>Cuscuta</i>. HGT sequences exist predominantly in functional protein-coding genes, sometimes as non-coding elements (such as repeats) or evolve gradually into pseudogenes following transfer.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                    
                  </li>
                  <li>
                    <span class="u-custom-list-number">(6)</span>
                    
                      <p>This study demonstrates that <i>Cuscuta</i> is unusually proficient at exchanging nucleic acids with its host. Taken together with mRNA movement between host and <i>C. campestris</i>, and the movement of miRNAs from <i>C. campestris</i> to host, this unprecedented number of HGT events in <i>Cuscuta</i> suggests there are few barriers to the exchange of material that was once thought to reside strictly in a single cell (note that this also includes exchange of proteins). We are increasingly aware of the roles of small RNAs and mRNA in the systemic transmission of signals within a single plant, so the prospect of such exchange occurring between plant species raises many questions. Our study argues strongly for a DNA mechanism of HGT, but offers little insight into the pathway taken by a genomic integrant. Is DNA itself mobile within a typical plant? Is a viral vector involved, or does transfer occur directly from plant to plant only in unusual circumstances, such as grafting or parasitism? In addition, the transfer of DNA between plants is only the first step in a potentially ongoing interaction. The retained DNA sequence may produce an mRNA that directly codes for a protein useful in that cell, or the mRNA may move systemically around the parasite or even back into the host (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#Fig5">5</a>). Additionally, the HGT may evolve into a miRNA-encoding sequence that can regulate host gene expression and thus facilitate parasitism (Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#Fig3">3</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#Fig5">5</a>). At each step the exchange of nucleic acids must confer an advantage to the parasite for the genes to be retained, expressed and evolving under constraint, long after the HGT event, so it would appear that <i>Cuscuta</i> and other parasites gain substantial benefit from acquiring host genes.</p>
                    
                  </li>
                </ol></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec11" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec11-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec11">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec11-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Phylogenomic reconstruction of <i>Cuscuta</i> gene trees</h3><p>A comprehensive de novo transcriptome dataset from <i>C. campestris</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Shahid, S. et al. MicroRNAs from the parasitic plant Cuscuta campestris target host messenger RNAs. Nature 553, 82â85 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d14571e2065">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Ranjan, A. et al. De novo assembly and characterization of the transcriptome of the parasitic weed dodder identifies genes associated with plant parasitism. Plant Physiol. 166, 1186â1199 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d14571e2068">17</a></sup> was used for protein-coding gene predictions. Two versions of de novo transcriptome assembly (one from ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Ranjan, A. et al. De novo assembly and characterization of the transcriptome of the parasitic weed dodder identifies genes associated with plant parasitism. Plant Physiol. 166, 1186â1199 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d14571e2072">17</a></sup> with the authorsâ names in the format (Cuscuta_pentagona_Ranjan_117086), the other from our own transcriptome assembly (with authorsâ names in the format, Cpent200559_cp73505_c0_seq1_1911x_f1)) were used to capture all the transcripts from their data. The first assembly will henceforth be denoted as âRanjan et al. assemblyâ, whereas our own assembly will be denoted as âcustom assemblyâ. The <i>AssemblyPostProcessing</i> pipeline in PlantTribes (v.1.0.2) (<a href="https://github.com/dePamphilis/PlantTribes">https://github.com/dePamphilis/PlantTribes</a>) was used to predict non-redundant sets of coding regions (&gt;200ânt) and their corresponding amino acid translations for both de novo transcriptome assemblies in the study using ESTScan (v.3.0.3)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Iseli, C., Jongeneel, C. V. &amp; Bucher, P. ESTScan: a program for detecting, evaluating, and reconstructing potential coding regions in EST sequences. In Proc. Int. Conf. Intell. Syst. Mol. Biol. 138â148 (1999)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d14571e2086">41</a></sup>. In total, 59,949âsequences from the custom assembly were then classified into 9,852âorthogroups with the 26âgenome orthogroup classifications. To increase the signal-to-noise ratio for HGT detection, we added an expanded set of potential donor genomes and additional Asteridae genomes that are closely related to <i>Cuscuta</i>. These 13âadditional genomes include <i>Ipomea trifida</i> (sweet potato line Mx23Hm)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Hirakawa, H. et al. Survey of genome sequences in a wild sweet potato, Ipomoea trifida (H. B. K.) G. Don. DNA Res. 22, 171â179 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d14571e2097">42</a></sup>, <i>Coffea canephora</i> (coffee)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Denoeud, F. et al. The coffee genome provides insight into the convergent evolution of caffeine biosynthesis. Science 345, 1181â1184 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d14571e2104">43</a></sup>, <i>Fraxinus excelsior</i> (ash)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Sollars, E. S. et al. Genome sequence and genetic diversity of European ash trees. Nature 541, 212â216 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d14571e2112">44</a></sup>, <i>Sesamum indicum</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Wang, L. et al. Genome sequencing of the high oil crop sesame provides insight into oil biosynthesis. Genome Biol. 15, R39 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d14571e2118">45</a></sup> (sesame), <i>Actinidia chinensis</i> (kiwifruit)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Huang, S. et al. Draft genome of the kiwifruit Actinidia chinensis. Nat. Commun. 4, 2640 (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d14571e2125">46</a></sup>, <i>Cynara cardunculus</i> (artichoke)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Scaglione, D. et al. The genome sequence of the outbreeding globe artichoke constructed de novo incorporating a phase-aware low-pass sequencing strategy of F1 progeny. Sci. Rep. 6, 19427 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d14571e2133">47</a></sup>, <i>Nicotiana benthamiana</i> (tobacco)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Sierro, N. et al. The tobacco genome sequence and its comparison with those of tomato and potato. Nat. Commun. 5, 3833 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d14571e2140">48</a></sup>, <i>Daucus carota</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Iorizzo, M. et al. A high-quality carrot genome assembly provides new insights into carotenoid accumulation and asterid genome evolution. Nat. Genet. 48, 657â666 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d14571e2146">49</a></sup> (carrot, Phytozome), <i>Amaranthus hypochondriacus</i> (princeâs feather, Phytozome), <i>Kalanchoe marnieriana</i> (Marnierâs kalanchoe, Phytozome), <i>Linum usitatissimum</i> (flax, Phytozome), <i>Capsella grandiflora</i> (grand shepherdâs purse, Phytozome) and <i>Eutrema salsugineum</i> (saltwater cress, Phytozome). Three Orobanchaceae parasitic plants were also includedâ<i>Triphysaria versicolor</i> (yellowbeak owlâs clover), <i>Striga hermonthica</i> (giant witchweed) and <i>Phelipanche aegyptiaca</i> (Egyptian broomrape)âwith transcriptome assemblies from our previous study<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Yang, Z. et al. Comparative transcriptome analyses reveal core parasitism genes and suggest gene duplication and repurposing as sources of structural novelty. Mol. Biol. Evol. 32, 767â790 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d14571e2176">50</a></sup> but with post-processing improvements as above. Genes from these 16âspecies were assigned to the 26-genome orthogroup classifications and used to build trees together with <i>C. campestris</i> sequences. Two versions of trees were generated, one by filtering out short sequences resulting in gaps in multiple sequence alignments, the other by retaining all sequences in each orthogroup. For the custom assembly, 9,782âtrees were generated for sequences from 9,852âorthogroups that contained at least four sequences. An additional 902âtrees were built using genes from the Ranjan et al. genome assembly<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Ranjan, A. et al. De novo assembly and characterization of the transcriptome of the parasitic weed dodder identifies genes associated with plant parasitism. Plant Physiol. 166, 1186â1199 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d14571e2183">17</a></sup> that were classified into a unique set of orthogroups compared to those from the custom assembly.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">HGT screening on phylogenetic trees</h3><p>Screening for HGT was performed using both large-scale phylogenetic trees and BLAST analyses. Phylogenomic screening of HGT followed the same approach employed in ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Yang, Z. et al. Horizontal gene transfer is more frequent with increased heterotrophy and contributes to parasite adaptation. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 113, E7010âE7019 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d14571e2195">7</a></sup>, but with improvements to enhance handling cases of HGT events involving more than one <i>Cuscuta</i> sequence. An improved schema (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM1">3</a>) of all possible HGT scenarios was designed to identify HGT from putative donors from distantly related rosids and distant asterids. Customized scripts were developed to identify nodes with HGT signal and placed at GitHub (<a href="https://github.com/dePamphilis/Cuscuta_HGT_ms_code">https://github.com/dePamphilis/Cuscuta_HGT_ms_code</a>). Further manual curation was performed for each orthogroup to improve the tree by increased taxon sampling to fix long branch artefacts or increase the taxon density when the HGT signal was weak.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">BLAST-based HGT screening</h3><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec15">HGTpropor</h4><p>We developed a new approach that identifies HGT-derived sequences based on BLAST analyses. This approach is reliant on the assumption that a vertically transmitted sequence will have a high proportion of hits from closely related taxa (close group), whereas a HGT-derived sequence will have a high proportion of hits from distantly related taxa (distant, or distal, group), and that often the hits are from one homogenous distal group. BLAST results were parsed by following several criteria: (1) genes with best non-self hit from non-plant sources were removed; (2) genes with five or fewer <i>Cuscuta</i>-only hits were removed; and (3) the remaining genes were examined by their Viridiplantae hits. The detailed procedure (involving manual curation, because some hits to the NCBI non-redundant protein collection (NR) lack taxonomic information such as order or phylum) on parsing of BLAST results from both NR and customized databases is available on GitHub (<a href="https://github.com/dePamphilis/Cuscuta_HGT_ms_code">https://github.com/dePamphilis/Cuscuta_HGT_ms_code</a>). Because the signal is concentrated mainly on the top hits, only the top 50âBLAST hits were examined. We termed this approach HGTpropor, due to its focus on a high proportion of top hits from distantly related taxa. A fairly complete HGT database was built that includes sequences from the NR and a manual selection of taxa (137âspecies) including 48âgenomes and 89âtranscriptomes (our own collection or taxa from the 1000 Plant Transcriptome Project<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Matasci, N. et al. Data access for the 1,000 Plants (1KP) project. Gigascience 3, 17 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d14571e2234">51</a></sup> and PlantGDB<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Duvick, J. et al. PlantGDB: a resource for comparative plant genomics. Nucleic Acids Res. 36, D959âD965 (2008)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d14571e2238">52</a></sup>) (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM3">17</a>). Customized scripts were developed to obtain taxonomic classification (species, genus, family, order, manually curated group) by parsing BLAST hits from both NR and our manually collected taxa. Each taxonomic order was classified into a group based on their relationship with the focal taxa (<i>C. campestris</i>)âself, close or distal group (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM3">18</a>). Classification distinguishes between a genus from self or close taxa and one from parasitic or non-parasitic taxa. A distal group was split into several smaller evolutionary lineages to decrease the noise. In this study, <i>Cuscuta</i> belongs to Convolvulaceae in the order Solanales, which can be either a self (if the hit is to <i>Cuscuta</i>) or close (if to another genus, such as <i>Convolvulus</i>, <i>Ipomoea</i> or <i>Solanum</i>). Sequences from any parasitic taxa in the top 50âhits were considered as potential HGT across parasitic plants (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM3">18</a>). A distal group was further classified into either distal rosid, distal asterid (Asterales, Apiales, Ericales, Berberidopsidales, Santalales and Caryophyllales), distal basal (basal eudicots and basal angiosperms), distal non-seed plant or distal gymnosperm (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM3">18</a>). Based on this classification, scripts were developed to produce a proportion for each gene from self, close and various distal groups (<a href="https://github.com/dePamphilis/Cuscuta_HGT_ms_code">https://github.com/dePamphilis/Cuscuta_HGT_ms_code</a>). An HGT candidate was characterized by the signature of a high proportion of hits from a distal group but low proportion from a close group. In our analyses, we applied a distal cut-off ratio of 0.7 and close cut-off of 0.2.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec16">HGTector</h4><p>HGTector, a previously published approach<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Zhu, Q., Kosoy, M. &amp; Dittmar, K. HGTector: an automated method facilitating genome-wide discovery of putative horizontal gene transfers. BMC Genom. 15, 717 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d14571e2289">53</a></sup> for identification of HGT from BLAST results, was also employed in our study. HGTector uses a normalized score by dividing each hitâs bitscore by the self bitscore. Then for each gene, the normalized bitscores from self, close and distal groups are summed to generate three measures for each gene. To decrease the noise from inaccurate open reading frame prediction, we kept BLAST results only for genes with greater than 20âhits. A density distribution was then plotted for each self, close and distal group using the measures for all genes. Cut-offs were determined by taking the midpoint between the first peak and valley from the distribution of self, close and distal groups. HGT genes were identified with a close weight smaller than the close cut-off but a distal weight greater than the distal cut-off. Inspired by our HGTpropor approach, we adapted it by splitting a distal group into several distal groups, requiring each taxon from a distal group to be closely related to the others.</p><p>Using a combined BLAST-based identification from HGTector and HGTpropor, we gathered a union of all HGT candidates from each approach (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM3">19</a>). Candidate HGT genes in <i>Cuscuta</i> were classified into 26-genome orthogroup classifications (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM3">20</a>), and gene trees were built for validation using the approach previously described (see Phylogenomic reconstruction of <i>Cuscuta</i> gene trees, above).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Validation of HGT candidates (protein-coding genes)</h3><p>Horizontal gene transfer candidates were validated with an automated tree reconstruction pipeline, plus a scoring system. In this round of validation, all 26âgenomes comprising the backbone of the orthogroup classification were included, along with additional genomes and transcriptomes. The additional genomes included 13âsequenced plant genomes (see Phylogenomic reconstruction of <i>Cuscuta</i> gene trees, above). The transcriptomes included three Orobanchaceae parasitic taxa described above (<i>T. versicolor</i>, <i>S. hermonthica</i> and <i>P. aegyptiaca</i>), with the aim of identifying HGT events that had occurred in more than one parasitic lineage; eight <i>Cuscuta</i> species with de novo transcriptome assemblies of one or two developmental stages from each taxon (<i>C. campestris</i> (haustoria on <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>), <i>C. chilensis</i> (haustoria on <i>Medicago sativa</i>), <i>Cuscuta europaea</i> (haustoria on <i>A. thaliana</i> and seeds), <i>Cuscuta exaltata</i> (seeds and seedlings), <i>Cuscuta harperi</i> (haustoria on <i>A. thaliana</i> and seedlings), <i>Cuscuta obtusiflora</i> (seeds and seedlings), <i>Cuscuta pentagona</i> (seedlings) and <i>Cuscuta rostrata</i> (haustoria on <i>A. thaliana</i>)); and 11âclosely related taxa, to increase the signal/noise ratio (<i>Atropa belladonna</i> (1,000 Plants database (1KP) project<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Matasci, N. et al. Data access for the 1,000 Plants (1KP) project. Gigascience 3, 17 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d14571e2377">51</a></sup>), <i>Convolvulus arvensis</i> (1KP), <i>Ipomoea indica</i> (1KP), <i>Ipomoea quamoclit</i> (1KP), <i>Olea europaea</i> (Trinity assembly of reads from 1KP), <i>Paulownia fargesil</i> (Trinity assembly of reads from 1KP), <i>Ipomoea nil</i> (plantGDB), <i>Petunia integrifolia</i> (plantGDB), <i>Physalis peruviana</i> (plantGDB), <i>Solanum melongena</i> (plantGDB) and <i>Wrightia tinctoria</i> (plantGDB)). The scoring criteria described in ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Yang, Z. et al. Horizontal gene transfer is more frequent with increased heterotrophy and contributes to parasite adaptation. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 113, E7010âE7019 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d14571e2413">7</a></sup> were employed to validate the confidence of HGTs in the final versions of the phylogenetic tree.</p><p>All medium- and high-confidence HGT orthogroup trees were retained and then subjected to a rigorous validation process. HGT sequences in <i>C. campestris</i> were first searched against the v.0.1 <i>C. campestris</i> assembly<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Shahid, S. et al. MicroRNAs from the parasitic plant Cuscuta campestris target host messenger RNAs. Nature 553, 82â85 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d14571e2426">15</a></sup> to confirm their presence in the genome. Sequences without BLASTn hits in the genome assembly were eliminated from further consideration. Second, sequences were searched with BLASTn against the NCBI non-redundant nucleotide database (NT)âto remove possible contamination from the experimental host. Third, candidate sequences, especially from phylogenomic screening, were searched with BLASTp against NR (downloaded from ftp://ftp.ncbi.nih.gov/blast/db on 7âOctober, 2015) to ensure that top BLASTp hits were from a distantly related donor (HGTpropor criteria: BLAST hits from closely related taxa were weaker than hits to distal taxa) and that the distal blast bitscore was significantly greater than the self bitscore (HGTector criteria<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Zhu, Q., Kosoy, M. &amp; Dittmar, K. HGTector: an automated method facilitating genome-wide discovery of putative horizontal gene transfers. BMC Genom. 15, 717 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d14571e2430">53</a></sup>). This is necessary because BLAST searches are exhaustive, whereas the donor inferred from the tree may be limited to species with sequenced genomes or good transcriptomes. Thus if top BLASTp hits of the HGT candidate match the tree-inferred donor or it is a close relative of the tree-inferred donor, it increases the confidence that the HGT candidate represents a true HGT. Finally, an expanded phylogenetic tree was built with sequences from the orthogroup containing the HGT sequences. At this stage, a final list of 105âtrue HGT OrthoMCL (v.2.0.9) groups was validated from the candidates predicted by a combination of BLAST and trees (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM3">21</a>). However, this set of 105âtrue HGT trees contains a largely redundant set of <i>Cuscuta</i> sequences because sequences were obtained from two independent <i>C. campestris</i> transcriptome assemblies, high-confidence Maker-P gene models from the 0.1 genome assembly (see section Genome assembly and annotation, below) and closely related copies detected from transcriptome assemblies in eight additional <i>Cuscuta</i> species.</p><p>Therefore, to further improve the trees for the final set of 105âHGT OrthoMCL groups, an OrthoFinder (v.1.1.2) classification was built by selecting 34ârepresentative genomes used for <i>Cuscuta</i> HGT identification (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM3">22</a>). Sequences from the 105âHGT OrthoMCL groups were cleaned and subjected to phylogenetic reconstruction with OrthoFinder groups. These OrthoFinder trees were used to validate the HGT events from the 105âHGT orthogroups.</p><p>The final tree was accepted as a high-confidence HGT only if it matched either schemeâ1 (HGT sequences nested within the donor clade with two strong supporting nodes) or schemeâ2 (HGT sequences sister to the donor clade) (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM1">3</a>), with two strong supporting nodes. When the HGT sequence grouped with a list of donor sequences but without high bootstrap support values, manual addition of more donor hits from a NR BLAST was used in an attempt to improve the tree.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Genome assembly and annotation</h3><p>The <i>C. campestris</i> genome was assembled with SOAPdenovo using 100-bp PE Illumina reads with insert sizes of 200âbp, 340âbp, 480âbp, 3âkb and 5âkb (after read cleaning and assembly, approximately 42Ãâdepth of nuclear genome). The de novo assembly was scaffolded and gap-filled using PBJelly (PBSuite v.15.2.20) with approximately 5Ãâcoverage of long Pacific Biosciences RSI-filtered sub-reads (8 SMRT cells), resulting in 56,350âscaffolds (â¥1âkb) with an N50 of 16.18âkb. We followed the protocol described in ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Campbell, M. S., Holt, C., Moore, B. &amp; Yandell, M. Genome annotation and curation using MAKER and MAKER-P. Curr. Protoc. Bioinformatics 48, 4.11.1â4.11.39 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d14571e2474">54</a></sup> to create a <i>C. campestris</i>-specific repeat library suitable for repeat masking before protein-coding gene annotation. Genes were predicted using the MAKER-P pipeline (v.2.31.8) with training sets incorporating curated plant proteins from SwissProt and <i>C. campestris</i> mRNA sequencing data from multiple tissue types including seed, seedling, stem, haustoria, flower and leaf. A total of 42,494âprotein-coding genes were predicted, consisting of all gene models supported by annotation evidence and gene models not supported by annotation evidence but encoding Pfam domains. The 0.1âgenome assembly and annotation are available at the Parasitic Plant Genome Project database (<a href="http://ppgp.huck.psu.edu/cuscuta.html">http://ppgp.huck.psu.edu/cuscuta.html</a>). A detailed description of the <i>C. campestris</i> genome assembly, species-specific repeat database construction and gene annotation will be reported elsewhere.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Selective constraint analyses</h3><p>Branch tests were performed following the procedure described in ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Yang, Z. et al. Horizontal gene transfer is more frequent with increased heterotrophy and contributes to parasite adaptation. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 113, E7010âE7019 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d14571e2503">7</a></sup>. For each of the 108âHGT events, HGT sequences from <i>C. campestris</i> and related <i>Cuscuta</i> species were used as the foreground, with the remaining sequences used as the background. The foreground and background lineages for each gene tree are clearly indicated on individual supplementary phylogenetic trees, with the foreground sequences indicated by â#1â (a link to pdf trees indicating the foreground and background is available at <a href="http://ppgp.huck.psu.edu/data/Cuscuta_HGT_Manuscript_Data/pdf_trees_with_foreground_labeled_for_constraint_analysis.zip">http://ppgp.huck.psu.edu/data/Cuscuta_HGT_Manuscript_Data/pdf_trees_with_foreground_labeled_for_constraint_analysis.zip</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">Ancestral reconstruction of HGT events</h3><p>Each of the HGT events was coded as one of two states: 0 (without or not detected HGT) or 1 (with HGT) (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM3">15</a>). Both Dollo and Wagner parsimony methods in the PHYLIP package (v.3.695)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Felsenstein, J. PHYLIP (Phylogenetic Inference Package), Version 3.6 Vol. 5 (Department of Genome Sciences, Univ. of Washington, 2005)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d14571e2531">55</a></sup> with default options (search for best tree-yes; Randomize input order of species-no; Use Threshold parsimony-no; Use ancestral states in input file-no; Sites weighted-no; Terminal type-ANSI) were used to infer the ancestral states of HGTs with an input tree for the eight sampled <i>Cuscuta</i> species, which was simplified from published phylogenies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Costea, M., GarcÃ­a, M. A., Baute, K. &amp; StefanoviÄ, S. Entangled evolutionary history of Cuscuta pentagona clade: a story involving hybridization and Darwin in the Galapagos. Taxon 64, 1225â1242 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d14571e2538">33</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec21">Bioinformatic search for homing introns</h3><p>Each of the 729âintrons contained in the HGT genes in this study was extracted from the <i>C. campestris</i> 0.1âgenome and further examined with BLASTp searches against databases NCBI/t, NCBI/nr, UniProt/SwissProt, UniProt/TrEMBL and TAIR10. Introns were also searched against a collection of protein family domain databases included in InterProScan (v.5.25.64.0) software and assigned with identified domains, which were also translated into gene ontology terms. The Interproscan command was: âinterproscan.sh -i &lt;INPUT FILE&gt; -f TSV -goterms -iprlookup âo &lt;OUTPUT FILE&gt; t -T temp -t pâ. We then queried the text for terms associated with sequences (homing, endonuclease, LAGLIDADG, I-PpoI and I-CreI. GIY-YIG, PD(D/E)xK, His-Cys Box, HNH, nicking enzyme, intein) that could be associated with homing capability<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Belfort, M. &amp; Bonocora, R. P. Homing endonucleases: from genetic anomalies to programmable genomic clippers. Methods Mol. Biol. 1123, 1â26 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d14571e2553">56</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Edgell, D. R. Selfish DNA: homing endonucleases find a home. Curr. Biol. 19, R115âR117 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d14571e2556">57</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec22">Genomic identification of HGT candidates using BLASTN</h3><p>The genome scaffolds of <i>C. campestris</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Shahid, S. et al. MicroRNAs from the parasitic plant Cuscuta campestris target host messenger RNAs. Nature 553, 82â85 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d14571e2570">15</a></sup> were interrogated to search for potential HGT-derived sequences. A BLASTn search was used because certain features, such as pseudogenes and non-coding elements, do not encode proteins and would be missed by a BLASTp-based approach and phylogenomics of predicted peptide sequences of genes. The BLASTn search started with 91,940âgenomic scaffolds resulting from the genome assembly against the latest NT database. The BLASTn commands used were: âblastn -task megablast -outfmt 6 -query input.fa -db nt_database -out input.blastn.nt -evalue 1e-5â. Next, BLAST results were processed by extraction of the GenInfo Identifier, which was converted to a taxonomic identification from each BLASTn hit. The command to convert GenInfo Identifier to taxonomy ID is: âefetch -id gi -db nucleotide -format docsum | xtract âpattern DocumentSummary -element TaxId &gt; outputâ<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Maglott, D., Ostell, J., Pruitt, K. D. &amp; Tatusova, T. Entrez Gene: gene-centered information at NCBI. Nucleic Acids Res. 39, D52âD57 (2011)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d14571e2574">58</a></sup>. Custom scripts using the âEntrez.efetchâ command were developed to export taxonomic information from each taxonomy ID. Taxonomic information includes scientific name, kingdom, subclass, order, family and genus. The tabular blast output was then concatenated with each hitâs taxonomic information. BLAST results were parsed to retain one significant HSP for each hit, and only Viridiplantae hits were retained. The order information of each hit was used to classify hits into self, close and distal groups. Subsequently, HGTpropor was applied to identify HGT candidates with a high proportion of hits from distal groups and a low proportion of hits from closely related groups. A distal cut-off of 0.9 was used to identify candidates, because our database contained only NT without the addition of sequences from closely related genomes of <i>Cuscuta</i>. Using this cut-off, 387, 27 and two HGT candidates greater than 300âbp from rosids, monocots and distal asterids were identified, respectively.</p><p>Furthermore, these candidates were evaluated in great detail for HGT validation. A large number of these candidates were identified as artefacts of vertically transmitted sequences, which was revealed by the addition of sequences from closely related genomes, in particular <i>Nicotiana</i>, <i>Solanum</i> and <i>Mimulus</i>. Remaining candidates were analysed with BLASTN and BLAST searches against NR and Phytozome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Goodstein, D. M. et al. Phytozome: a comparative platform for green plant genomics. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, D1178âD1186 (2012)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d14571e2593">59</a></sup>. Online BLAST searches against the NR database used a word size of 16 rather than the default value of 28. Otherwise, the BLAST hits can occasionally be misleading (BLASTp searches of many vertically transmitted <i>Cuscuta</i> sequences show top hits from closely related asterid species, yet BLASTn default searches show that top hits are from monocots; word size adjustment in BLASTn allowed the discovery of additional close sequences. This represents how many of the monocot-derived HGT candidates were found to be artefacts, one of which finally proved to be a HGT from a legume donor rather than from a monocot (onion). We found no HGTs from grasses in <i>Cuscuta</i>, a result corroborated by the protein-coding sequences.</p><p>In terms of rosid-derived HGT candidates, when BLAST hit distributions against NR are similar to those from Phytozome (v.10.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Goodstein, D. M. et al. Phytozome: a comparative platform for green plant genomics. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, D1178âD1186 (2012)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d14571e2607">59</a></sup> and when separate BLAST against the Sol Genomics Network<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Fernandez-Pozo, N. et al. The Sol Genomics Network (SGN)âfrom genotype to phenotype to breeding. Nucleic Acids Res. 43, D1036âD1041 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d14571e2611">60</a></sup> yields far lower bitscores than from distal rosids hits from NR (the empirical cut-off for close hit bitscore is 80% lower than the distal bitscore), the HGT candidate is likely to be validated as it also has support from HGTpropor. Next, these candidates were validated with BLASTx to ensure a BLAST output similar to BLASTn. The use of BLASTn and BLASTx can further classify the candidates into protein-coding or non-coding candidates. If the top BLASTn hits of a HGT candidate encode proteins, and when further BLASTx or BLASTp yields hits of similar sequences, these HGT candidates were classified as âprotein-coding genesâ. The HGT sequences were then aligned against donor sequences to examine whether they contained premature stop codons. HGT sequences that contained premature stop codons while their donor encoded a functional protein were classified as âHGT-derived pseudogenesâ. On the other hand, if BLASTn hits of HGT sequences were repeats or non-protein-coding features and BLASTx did not yield significant hits, these candidates were likely to be considered as repeats. The non-coding nature of the HGT sequences was further validated when a Phytozome BLASTn against the genome of the top blast hit species yielded the best BLAST hit annotated as ârepeatsâ or non-genes. As an additional check, all 203âhigh-confidence HGT regions were aligned to an independently generated <i>C. campestris</i> genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Vogel, A. et al. Footprints of parasitism in the genome of the parasitic flowering plant Cuscuta campestris. Nat. Commun. 9, 2515 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d14571e2618">12</a></sup>; 193 or 203 (95%) of the sequences were also found in that genome with alignments of at least 250âbp and 95% or greater sequence identity (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM1">1</a>). The results of the final list of HGT sequences, and whether they encode proteins or repeats, are shown in Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM3">23</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec23">Identifying small RNA enrichment of <i>C. campestris</i> HGT genes</h3><p>To find HGT genes enriched with small RNAs, we utilized de novo annotated <i>C. campestris</i> small RNA loci from a previously published study<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Shahid, S. et al. MicroRNAs from the parasitic plant Cuscuta campestris target host messenger RNAs. Nature 553, 82â85 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d14571e2644">15</a></sup>. We also obtained the expression and alignment data of these loci for parasite stem, haustoria and host <i>A. thaliana</i> stem libraries (two biological replicates per sample) from the same study. Only <i>C. campestris</i> small RNA loci predominantly expressing 20â24-nt RNAs (13,809âloci) were utilized for all subsequent analyses. strucVis (v.0.3; <a href="https://github.com/MikeAxtell/strucVis">https://github.com/MikeAxtell/strucVis</a>) was used to visualize small RNA sequencing read coverage along the predicted secondary structures of annotated <i>C. campestris MIRNAs</i>. For generation of expression heat maps, raw read count for each of the small RNA loci was normalized based on the total number of processed reads for each of the libraries.</p><p>Overlap of <i>C. campestris</i> small RNA loci against 200âprotein-coding HGT genes annotated in the <i>C. campestris</i> v.0.1 genome (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM3">13</a>) was determined using bedtools v.2.22.0 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Quinlan, A. R. &amp; Hall, I. M. BEDTools: a flexible suite of utilities for comparing genomic features. Bioinformatics 26, 841â842 (2010)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d14571e2677">61</a></sup>) with default settings. Ten independent cohorts of random <i>C. campestris v0.1</i> genes equal to the number of HGT genes (that is, 200âgenes) were also generated for testing small RNA enrichment of random genes. For each of these cohorts, <i>C. campestris v0.1</i> genes overlapping annotated small RNA loci were identified using bedtools as described above.</p><p><i>C. campestris v0.1</i> parent locus coordinates of the HGT genes was used as reference loci for quantifying the total number and predominant size of small RNAs per HGT gene, using ShortStack v.3.8.4 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Johnson, N. R., Yeoh, J. M., Coruh, C. &amp; Axtell, M. J. Improved placement of multi-mapping small RNAs. G3 (Bethesda) 6, 2103â2111 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d14571e2693">62</a></sup>) with previously reported alignment data<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Shahid, S. et al. MicroRNAs from the parasitic plant Cuscuta campestris target host messenger RNAs. Nature 553, 82â85 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d14571e2697">15</a></sup>. The ShortStack commands were: âShortStack âlocifile &lt;HGT_loci.txt&gt;âbamfile &lt;alignments.bam&gt;âgenomefile &lt;merged_ath167_cpv0.1_genome.fasta&gt;â.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec24">Reporting Summary</h3><p>Further information on research design is available in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM2">Nature Research Reporting Summary</a> linked to this article.</p></div></div></section>
                

                <section aria-labelledby="data-availability" data-title="Data availability"><div class="c-article-section" id="data-availability-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="data-availability">Data availability</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="data-availability-content">
              
              <p>Publicly available data sources are as given in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#Sec11">Methods</a> section of the manuscript. Web links for publicly available datasets are indicated in Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM3">22</a>. <i>C. campestris</i> genome assembly and annotations are available from <a href="http://ppgp.huck.psu.edu/cuscuta.html">http://ppgp.huck.psu.edu/cuscuta.html</a>. The raw sequence reads for the eight <i>Cuscuta</i> taxa sampled in this study (<i>Cuscuta</i> species RNA sequencing datasets), <i>C. campestris</i> HGT sequences, all multiple sequence alignments and HGT tree files, as well as the supporting trees and alignments for selective constraint analyses (<i>C. campestris</i> HGT gene sequences, alignments and phylogenies), are given as supporting data at <a href="http://ppgp.huck.psu.edu/data/Cuscuta_HGT_Manuscript_Data/">http://ppgp.huck.psu.edu/data/Cuscuta_HGT_Manuscript_Data/</a>. All HGT sequences extracted from these assemblies are included as supporting data in the posted multiple sequence alignments and as described below. The raw data for Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#Fig1">1a</a> are in Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM3">2</a> (column C); Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#Fig1">1b</a> in Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0458-0#MOESM3">3</a>;             </div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="code-availability" data-title="Code availability"><div class="c-article-section" id="code-availability-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="code-availability">Code availability</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="code-availability-content">
              
              <p>The customized code and pipeline associated with data analysis are available from <a href="https://github.com/dePamphilis/Cuscuta_HGT_ms_code">https://github.com/dePamphilis/Cuscuta_HGT_ms_code</a>.</p>
